<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Gradual polyploid genome evolution revealed by pan-genomic analysis of Brachypodium hybridum and its diploid progenitors"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-07-29"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 This is a U.S. government work and not under copyright protection in the U.S.; foreign copyright protection may apply"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Our understanding of polyploid genome evolution is constrained because we cannot know the exact founders of a&#160;particular polyploid. To differentiate between founder effects and post polyploidization evolution, we use a pan-genomic approach to study the allotetraploid Brachypodium hybridum and its diploid progenitors. Comparative analysis suggests that most B. hybridum whole gene presence/absence variation is part of the standing variation in its diploid progenitors. Analysis of nuclear single nucleotide variants, plastomes and k-mers associated with retrotransposons reveals two independent origins for B. hybridum, ~1.4 and ~0.14 million years ago. Examination of gene expression in the younger B. hybridum lineage reveals no bias in overall subgenome expression. Our results are consistent with a gradual accumulation of genomic changes after polyploidization and a lack of subgenome expression dominance. Significantly, if we did not use a pan-genomic approach, we would grossly overestimate the number of genomic changes attributable to post polyploidization evolution. Existing plant pan-genomic studies usually report considerable intraspecific whole gene presence-absence variation. Here, the authors use pan-genomic approach to reveal gradual polyploid genome evolution by analyzing of Brachypodium hybridum and its diploid progenitors."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6287-2697"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-6287-2697</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="DOE Joint Genome Institute" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0449 479X, grid.451309.a, DOE Joint Genome Institute, Berkeley, CA, 94720, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Daniel-Rokhsar" data-author-popup="auth-Daniel-Rokhsar">Daniel Rokhsar</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-8704-2224"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-8704-2224</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="DOE Joint Genome Institute" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0449 479X, grid.451309.a, DOE Joint Genome Institute, Berkeley, CA, 94720, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University California, Berkeley" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2181 7878, grid.47840.3f, University California, Berkeley, Berkeley, CA, 94720, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jeremy-Schmutz" data-author-popup="auth-Jeremy-Schmutz">Jeremy Schmutz</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8062-9172"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-8062-9172</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff14">14</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="DOE Joint Genome Institute" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0449 479X, grid.451309.a, DOE Joint Genome Institute, Berkeley, CA, 94720, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="HudsonAlpha Institute for Biotechnology" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0408 3720, grid.417691.c, HudsonAlpha Institute for Biotechnology, Huntsville, AL, 35806, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Robert-Hasterok" data-author-popup="auth-Robert-Hasterok">Robert Hasterok</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6667-2721"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-6667-2721</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff12">12</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Silesia in Katowice" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2259 4135, grid.11866.38, Plant Cytogenetics and Molecular Biology Group, Institute of Biology, Biotechnology and Environmental Protection, Faculty of Natural Sciences, University of Silesia in Katowice, 40-032, Katowice, Poland" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Pilar-Catalan" data-author-popup="auth-Pilar-Catalan" data-corresp-id="c1">Pilar Catalan<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-7793-5259"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-7793-5259</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a>,<a href="#Aff10">10</a>,<a href="#Aff16">16</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Grupo de Bioquímica, Biofísica y Biología Computacional (BIFI, UNIZAR), Unidad Asociada al CSIC" /><meta itemprop="address" content="Grupo de Bioquímica, Biofísica y Biología Computacional (BIFI, UNIZAR), Unidad Asociada al CSIC, Zaragoza, Spain" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Universidad de Zaragoza-Escuela Politécnica Superior de Huesca" /><meta itemprop="address" content="Universidad de Zaragoza-Escuela Politécnica Superior de Huesca, 22071, Huesca, Spain" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Tomsk State University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 1088 3909, grid.77602.34, Institute of Biology, Tomsk State University, Tomsk, 634050, Russia" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-John_P_-Vogel" data-author-popup="auth-John_P_-Vogel" data-corresp-id="c2">John P. Vogel<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-1786-2689"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-1786-2689</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="DOE Joint Genome Institute" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0449 479X, grid.451309.a, DOE Joint Genome Institute, Berkeley, CA, 94720, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University California, Berkeley" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2181 7878, grid.47840.3f, University California, Berkeley, Berkeley, CA, 94720, USA" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Our understanding of polyploid genome evolution is constrained because we cannot know the exact founders of a particular polyploid. To differentiate between founder effects and post polyploidization evolution, we use a pan-genomic approach to study the allotetraploid <i>Brachypodium hybridum</i> and its diploid progenitors. Comparative analysis suggests that most <i>B. hybridum</i> whole gene presence/absence variation is part of the standing variation in its diploid progenitors. Analysis of nuclear single nucleotide variants, plastomes and k-mers associated with retrotransposons reveals two independent origins for <i>B. hybridum</i>, ~1.4 and ~0.14 million years ago. Examination of gene expression in the younger <i>B. hybridum</i> lineage reveals no bias in overall subgenome expression. Our results are consistent with a gradual accumulation of genomic changes after polyploidization and a lack of subgenome expression dominance. Significantly, if we did not use a pan-genomic approach, we would grossly overestimate the number of genomic changes attributable to post polyploidization evolution.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Polyploidy is a major evolutionary force-shaping eukaryotic genomes, particularly in the flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Van De Peer, Y., Mizrachi, E. &amp; Marchal, K. The evolutionary significance of polyploidy. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 411–424 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1052">1</a></sup>. Indeed, the origin of all flowering plants can be traced back to a polyploidization event shortly before the emergence of flowers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97–100 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1056">2</a></sup>. Thus, all flowering plants contain at least one polyploidization event in their evolutionary history and some, including the model plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, contain several<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Paterson, A. H., Bowers, J. E. &amp; Chapman, B. A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903–9908 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1063">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1066">4</a></sup>. Over evolutionary time, polyploid genomes gradually revert to a functionally diploid state, by processes that include the shedding of redundant sequences. This process is still underway today, and 30–70% of all flowering plants are relatively recent polyploids whose genomes still contain multiple copies of many genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Soltis, P. S., Marchant, D. B., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Polyploidy and genome evolution in plants. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 35, 119–125 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1070">5</a></sup>. Included among these more recent polyploids are the species that feed the world (e.g., wheat, potato, sugarcane) and species that are increasingly being called upon as sources of renewable fuel (e.g., switchgrass). The pervasiveness of polyploidy indicates that it must be evolutionarily favorable, presumably because the sudden combination of genomes and the presence of duplicates for virtually every gene allows neofunctionalization and subfunctionalization as well as other benefits such as fixed heterosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Comai, L. The advantages and disadvantages of being polyploid. Nat. Rev. Genet. 6, 836–846 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1075">6</a></sup>. In addition to its evolutionary role, polyploidy has been widely exploited in agriculture, and breeders have used artificially induced polyploidy to increase fruit and flower size (e.g., strawberry), create more adaptable and drought resistant crops (e.g., triticale) and produce seedless fruit (e.g., watermelon).</p><p>Despite the importance of polyploidy, detailed understanding of the temporal dynamics of genome evolution after polyploidization is still unknown. The current paradigm includes possible rapid epigenetic reprogramming, changes in gene expression, transposon activation, and altered gene splicing. Over longer timescales, changes in DNA sequence can lead to neofunctionalization, gene conversion, deletion or pseudogenization of nonessential genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Functional divergence of duplicated genes formed by polyploidy during Arabidopsis evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 16, 1679–1691 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1082">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Freeling, M. et al. Fractionation mutagenesis and similar consequences of mechanisms removing dispensable or less-expressed DNA in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 15, 131–139 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1085">8</a></sup>. In addition, dosage balance for some genes is expected to shape the genome at every stage via several mechanisms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Conant, G. C., Birchler, J. A. &amp; Pires, J. C. Dosage, duplication, and diploidization: clarifying the interplay of multiple models for duplicate gene evolution over time. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 19, 91–98 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1089">9</a></sup>. However, the precise order, timescale and the underlying mechanisms of these events are largely unknown<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Ancient WGD events as drivers of key innovations in angiosperms. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 30, 159–165 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1093">10</a></sup>.</p><p>Further complicating our understanding of polyploid genome evolution is the high level of intraspecific genomic variation that has been detected by recent pan-genomic studies. Indeed, pan-genomes for several plant species have been shown to be much larger than the genomes of any individual line<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gan, X. et al. Multiple reference genomes and transcriptomes for Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 477, 419–423 (2011)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1100">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045–1052 (2014)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1100_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Insights into the maize pan-genome and pan-transcriptome. Plant Cell 26, 121–135 (2014)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1100_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yao, W. et al. Exploring the rice dispensable genome using a metagenome-like assembly strategy. Genome Biol. 16, 187 (2015)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1100_3">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Extensive gene content variation in the Brachypodium distachyon pan-genome correlates with population structure. Nat. Commun. 8, 1–13 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1103">15</a></sup>. Thus, the standing variation in a polyploid species is the sum of the variation present in the parental genomes and the variation that developed after polyploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Soltis, D. E., Visger, C. J., Marchant, D. B. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Polyploidy: pitfalls and paths to a paradigm. Am. J. Bot. 103, 1146–1166 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1107">16</a></sup>. This has important implications for our interpretation of polyploid genome evolution. If a polyploid species is formed from a single-hybridization event, then all the genomic diversity within that species will be due to sequence changes after the hybridization event. However, if the polyploid species was formed by multiple hybridizations, then the diversity would be the sum of the diversity in all the parents and the sequence changes that arose after the hybridization events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Doyle, J. J. &amp; Sherman-Broyles, S. Double trouble: taxonomy and definitions of polyploidy. N. Phytol. 213, 487–493 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1111">17</a></sup>. In both scenarios, comparison of a single polyploid genome to single genomes of the diploid progenitor species will overestimate the variation due to evolution after the polyploidization event because of intraspecific differences between the reference diploid lines and the actual diploid parents of the polyploid. To overcome this limitation, it is necessary to use a pan-genomic approach. Unfortunately, polyploid biomass and grain crops are difficult experimental subjects because of their large, complex genomes; large physical size; and, for biomass crops, their typically outbreeding nature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Vogel, J. The rise of Brachypodium as a model system. in Genetics and Genomics of Brachypodium. (ed. Vogel, J.) 1–7 (Springer, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1115">18</a></sup>. Therefore, we use a model grass polyploid system consisting of the diploids <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> and <i>B. stacei</i>, and their derived allotetraploid <i>B. hybridum</i>. <i>B. distachyon</i> has emerged as a powerful model to study grass biology, and numerous experimental resources have been developed for it<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Scholthof, K. B. G., Irigoyen, S., Catalan, P. &amp; Mandadi, K. K. Brachypodium: a monocot grass model genus for plant biology. Plant Cell 30, 1673–1694 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1132">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhu, T. et al. Analysis of Brachypodium genomes with genome-wide optical maps. Genome 61, 559–565 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1135">20</a></sup>. Historically, <i>B. distachyon</i> included the current <i>B. distachyon</i>, as well as <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. hybridum</i>. This complex was reclassified as three separate species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Catalán, P. et al. Evolution and taxonomic split of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Ann. Bot. 109, 385–405 (2012)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1152">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Catalán, P., López-Alvarez, D., Díaz-Pérez, A., Sancho, R. &amp; López-Herranz, M. Phylogeny and evolution of the genus Brachypodium. in Genetics and genomics of Brachypodium (ed. Vogel, J.) 9–38 (Springer, 2016)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1152_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="López-Álvarez, D., Zubair, H., Beckmann, M., Draper, J. &amp; Catalán, P. Diversity and association of phenotypic and metabolomic traits in the close model grasses Brachypodium distachyon, B. stacei and B. hybridum. Ann. Bot. 119, 545–561 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1155">23</a></sup>. Experimental crosses demonstrated that the three species are reproductively isolated from each other<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Catalán, P., López-Alvarez, D., Díaz-Pérez, A., Sancho, R. &amp; López-Herranz, M. Phylogeny and evolution of the genus Brachypodium. in Genetics and genomics of Brachypodium (ed. Vogel, J.) 9–38 (Springer, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1159">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Thi, V. H. D. et al. Recreating stable Brachypodium hybridum allotetraploids by uniting the divergent genomes of B. distachyon and B. stacei. PLoS ONE 11, e0167171 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1162">24</a></sup>. <i>B. hybridum</i> has subgenomes designated D and S that are derived from <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. stacei</i>, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Catalan, P. et al. Update on the genomics and basic biology of Brachypodium. International Brachypodium Initiative (IBI). Trends Plant Sci. 19, 414–418 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1176">25</a></sup>. Because some <i>B. hybridum</i> lines have chloroplast genomes (plastomes) derived from <i>B. stacei</i> and some have plastomes derived from <i>B. distachyon</i>, we know that <i>B. hybridum</i> formed at least twice from reciprocal crosses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Catalán, P., López-Alvarez, D., Díaz-Pérez, A., Sancho, R. &amp; López-Herranz, M. Phylogeny and evolution of the genus Brachypodium. in Genetics and genomics of Brachypodium (ed. Vogel, J.) 9–38 (Springer, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1193">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Díaz-Pérez, A., López-Álvarez, D., Sancho, R. &amp; Catalán, P. Reconstructing the origins and the biogeography of species’ genomes in the highly reticulate allopolyploid-rich model grass genus Brachypodium using minimum evolution, coalescence and maximum likelihood approaches. Molecular Phylogenetics and Evolution 127, 256–271 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1196">26</a></sup>. <i>Brachypodium hybridum</i> lines are designated as D plastotype if their plastome is derived from <i>B. distachyon</i> or S plastotype if their plastome is derived from <i>B. stacei</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig1">1</a>). All three species have very compact genomes (272, 234, and 509 Mb, respectively), small stature, and are genetically transformable and easily grown and manipulated in the laboratory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Scholthof, K. B. G., Irigoyen, S., Catalan, P. &amp; Mandadi, K. K. Brachypodium: a monocot grass model genus for plant biology. Plant Cell 30, 1673–1694 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1212">19</a></sup>. In addition, as wild species their genomes have not been impacted by selection and genetic bottlenecks during domestication.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Brachypodium hybridum subgenome and plastotype naming convention."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: <i>Brachypodium hybridum</i> subgenome and plastotype naming convention.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="373" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><i>Brachypodium hybridum</i> has formed more than once from reciprocal crosses. We can use chloroplast genome (plastome) sequence to determine the maternal progenitor species of any <i>B. hybridum</i> line. To facilitate writing about the origin of individual lines, we designate lines that had <i>B. distachyon</i> as a maternal progenitor species as D plastotype, and lines that had <i>B. stacei</i> as a maternal progenitor species as S plastotype. The chromosomes of <i>B. distachyon</i> are named Bd1 to Bd5, and the chromosomes of <i>B. stacei</i> are named Bs1 to Bs10. To allow us to easily link <i>B. hybridum</i> chromosomes to their diploid orthologs, we named <i>B. hybridum</i> chromosomes BhD1 to BhD5 for chromosomes derived from <i>B. distachyon</i> and BhS1 to BhS10 for chromosomes derived from <i>B. stacei</i>. The diagram shows stylized plant cells with color coding, indicating the origin of the plastome and nuclear genomes (<i>B. distachyon</i>-type, blue; <i>B. stacei</i>-type, red). For clarity, the haploid number of chromosomes is shown.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In this study, we compare multiple genomes to unveil two allopolyploidization events separated by over a million years that led to the same speciation outcome. We compare the D subgenomes of <i>B. hybridum</i> lines to the <i>B. distachyon</i> pan-genome to reveal that the vast majority of whole-gene presence/absence variation in <i>B. hybridum</i> is part of the standing variation of <i>B. distachyon</i>, indicating that even after 1.4 million years the process of post-polyploidization gene loss is still in its infancy. In contrast, we detect large numbers of small polymorphisms, SNVs (here we define SNV as a single-nucleotide variant, either within or between species, at a syntenically conserved position) and small insertions/deletions unique to each <i>B. hybridum</i> lineage, with much greater numbers present in the older lineage. This paints a picture of steady accumulation of small sequence changes, and very gradual gene loss during the first 1.4 million years after polyploidization in <i>B. hybridum</i>. Notably, if we compared a single polyploid genome to individual genomes of the diploid progenitors, we would reach very different conclusions.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Nuclear genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>In this study, we created chromosome-scale reference genomes for <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. hybridum</i>. We also created seven additional <i>B. hybridum</i> genome assemblies, including a high-quality PacBio-based assembly, and Illumina assemblies for six additional <i>B. hybridum</i> lines (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM5">1</a>). In addition, we constructed Illumina-based assemblies for 57 <i>B. distachyon</i> lines (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM5">1</a>). The <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 genome was assembled from Illumina sequences using the ALLPATHS assembler. Contigs were ordered and oriented using a 22,043 marker genetic map to create ten chromosomes (named Bs1–Bs10) containing 234 Mb of sequence (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM6">2</a>). The <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 reference genome was assembled similar to <i>B. stacei</i>, except the Meraculous assembler was used and additional improvement was done with Dovetail Genomic’s Chicago<sup>TM</sup> library and HiRise<sup>TM</sup> assembler followed by final orientation using a 45,197 marker genetic map (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM7">3</a>). The chromosomes were named BhDN or BhSN where D or S refers to the <i>B. distachyon-</i>like or <i>B. stacei</i>-like subgenomes, respectively, and N stands for progenitor chromosome number (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig1">1</a>). The final assembly contains 99.4% (500.8/503.8 Mb) of all assembled sequence. The <i>B. hybridum</i> Bd28 genome was assembled from ~60× depth PacBio sequence using the Falcon assembler (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM5">1</a>). The 765 contigs were ordered and oriented into 15 chromosomes based on synteny to <i>B. distachyon</i> (Bd21 v.3.1), <i>B. stacei</i> (ABR114 v.1.1), and <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM5">1</a>). The euchromatic portion of the genome assemblies was shown to be nearly complete by aligning RNA-seq (99% read alignment) and by BUSCO scores (~99%).</p><p>In addition to the high-quality genome assemblies, we created lower-quality assemblies for six <i>B. hybridum</i> lines using &gt;60 × 250 bp Illumina paired-end reads (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM5">1</a>). Genome assembly was performed with the Meraculous assembler. The initial scaffolds were binned into D and S subgenomes via the seal tool in bbtools (v37) using <i>B. distachyon</i> (v. 3.1) and <i>B. stacei</i> (v. 1.1). Similarly, we assembled genomes for 57 <i>B. distachyon</i> inbred lines from 250 bp paired-end Illumina reads (&gt;50× coverage) from fragment insert libraries using the massively parallel HipMer genome assembler (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM5">1</a>). Including 42 previously sequenced <i>B. distachyon</i> lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Extensive gene content variation in the Brachypodium distachyon pan-genome correlates with population structure. Nat. Commun. 8, 1–13 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1394">15</a></sup>, a total of 99 <i>B. distachyon</i> lines with fully assembled genomes were used to produce whole-genome multiple sequence alignments (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>).</p><p>Assemblies from <i>B. hybridum</i> lines ABR113, Bd28, Bhyb30, Bhyb26, and Bhyb118-5; <i>B. distachyon</i> lines CSR-6 and LPA3.2; and <i>B. stacei</i> line ABR114 were annotated using the JGI annotation pipeline. Annotation metrics are listed in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM5">1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Synteny and karyotype analysis</h3><p>We compared the genomes of <i>Oryza sativa</i> v7 (Phytozome, proteome id: 323), <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114, <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 v3.1, and <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 to examine chromosome structure and identify large-scale rearrangements in the <i>B. hybridum</i> subgenomes. Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig2">2a, b</a> shows that nested insertions of whole chromosomes, like the ones in <i>B. stacei</i>, into centromeric regions formed <i>B. distachyon</i> chromosomes Bd1–Bd3, as was noted previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Vogel, J. P. et al. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1452">27</a></sup>. In addition, Bd4 is syntenic with two <i>B. stacei</i> chromosomes, Bs5 and Bs10 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig2">2a, b</a>), and three rice chromosomes, Os9, Os11, and Os12 (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig2">2</a>c and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig3">3</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Vogel, J. P. et al. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1469">27</a></sup>. Synteny suggests that Bs5 and Bs10 resulted either from the fission of a larger chromosome similar to Bd4 or by a reciprocal translocation followed by fusion of two ancestral chromosomes (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig2">2</a>b and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig3">3</a>). By contrast, Bd5 is highly collinear with chromosome Bs9, which in turn derives from large and small portions of Os4 and Os2, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig2">2a–d</a>). Comparison of <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i> chromosomes to the corresponding BhS and BhD chromosomes in <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 revealed almost perfect syntenic conservation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig2">2e, f</a>). This indicates that there were no major rearrangements in the subgenomes after polyploidization.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Syntenic relationships."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Syntenic relationships.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1417" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Synteny between chromosomes, represented by colors, indicates that the <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 chromosomes were derived from an ancestor similar to <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 through a series of nested insertions of whole chromosomes into centromeric regions in Bd1, Bd2, and Bd3, fusion of two chromosomes to Bd4, and full collinearity of one chromosome with Bd5. Note that the genomes of <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 and <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 are essentially 100% collinear with the S and D subgenomes of <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113, respectively. <b>b</b>–<b>f</b> Circos plots showing syntenic relationships between the above-mentioned genomes and subgenomes. Note the high conservation in synteny between <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 and <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 D subgenome (<b>e</b>) and between <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 and <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 S subgenome (<b>f</b>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Chromosome evolution inferred from cytomolecular analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Chromosome evolution inferred from cytomolecular analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="433" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Color-coded representation of the syntenic relationship between the five <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 chromosomes and the corresponding <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 chromosomes and ancestral rice chromosome equivalents. <b>a</b>–<b>c</b> The relationship between <i>B. distachyon</i> chromosomes Bd1 to Bd3 and the corresponding <i>B. stacei</i> chromosomes can be explained by a series of nested insertions of whole ancestral chromosome equivalents into centromeric regions (Bd1: Bs7 → Bs6 → Bs2; Bd2: Bs8 → Bs1; Bd3: Bs3 → Bs4). <b>d</b> Chromosome Bd4 shows the synteny of chromosomes Bs5 and Bs10 with the occurrence of a pericentromeric inversion in Bd4. <b>e</b> Chromosome Bd5 is highly collinear with Bs9. The asterisks indicate the location of the secondary constriction/satellite region in Bd5 and Bs6, which carry 35S rDNA loci.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We conducted molecular cytogenetic analysis to link the <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. hybridum</i> pseudomolecules to recently published karyotypes and validate the synteny analysis. Comparative chromosome barcoding using 80 low-repeat <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 BAC clones confirmed the synteny analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">1</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">1</a>). The <i>B. stacei</i> chromosome numbers of the pseudomolecules differ from the numbers assigned by a recent karyotype because the pseudomolecules were numbered according to assembled length and the karyotype designations were based on optical characteristics (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">2</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Lusinska, J. et al. Chromosome identification and reconstruction of evolutionary rearrangements in Brachypodium distachyon, B. stacei and B. hybridum. Ann. Bot. 10, 445–459 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1609">28</a></sup>. In this paper, we use the <i>B. stacei</i> chromosome numbers assigned to the pseudomolecules.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Plastome sequencing</h3><p>We assembled and annotated the plastomes of eight <i>B. hybridum</i> lines, 99 <i>B. distachyon</i> lines, and one <i>B. stacei</i> line (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>). Two of the eight <i>B. hybridum</i> lines (Bhyb26 and Bhyb118-5) had <i>B. distachyon</i>-type plastomes (D plastotype), whereas the remaining <i>B. hybridum</i> lines had <i>B. stacei</i>-type plastomes (S plastotype) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig1">1</a>). Plastome lengths varied from 135,031–135,423 bp in <i>B. distachyon</i> and the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype lines, and from 136,325–136,329 bp in <i>B. stacei</i> and the <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype lines (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">2</a>). All <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype plastomes and <i>B. stacei</i> showed a characteristic ~1.1 kb insertion in the LSC region and one rps19 deletion in the IRb repeat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Sancho, R. et al. Comparative plastome genomics and phylogenomics of Brachypodium: flowering time signatures, introgression and recombination in recently diverged ecotypes. New Phytol. 218, 1631–1644 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1674">29</a></sup>. Noticeably, the <i>B. stacei</i> and all <i>B. hybridum</i> plastomes (D and S plastotypes) shared several insertions and common SNVs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Nuclear phylogenomics and genetic structure</h3><p>Phylogenomic analysis was conducted with eight <i>B. hybridum</i> lines (16 subgenomes), 99 <i>B. distachyon</i> lines and one <i>B. stacei</i> line (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">4</a>) using 745,858 orthologous nucleotide positions (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM9">5</a>). These were selected because they were polymorphic (SNVs) between at least one pair of <i>B. distachyon</i> lines. Fifteen percent (114,694 of 745,858) of these were polymorphic (SNVs) between at least one pair of <i>B. stacei</i>/S subgenomes.</p><p>The best maximum-likelihood tree contained a main split between <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i>, and the root placement of this split was confirmed using a subset of 5443 nucleotide positions for which we could identify orthologous loci in <i>Oryza sativa</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a</a>; Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">6</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM10">6</a>). Fourteen percent of these (779 of 5443) were polymorphic (SNVs) between at least one pair of <i>B. stacei</i>/S subgenomes. As expected, the S subgenomes of <i>B. hybridum</i> grouped with <i>B. stacei</i> and the D subgenomes with the <i>B. distachyon</i> lines. The D and S subgenomes from both <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype lines, Bhyb26 and Bhyb118-5, formed clades that were sisters to the rest of the D subgenomes/<i>B. distachyon</i> genomes and the rest of the S subgenomes/<i>B. stacei</i> genomes, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">6</a>). The D subgenomes of the S-plastotype <i>B. hybridum</i> lines formed a clade nested within the extremely delayed flowering (EDF+) group of <i>B. distachyon</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">6</a>). Together this suggests that the D-plastotype lineage either formed before the radiation of the existing <i>B. distachyon</i> diversity or that the parents of the D-plastotype lineage belong to an unsampled group of <i>B. distachyon</i>. With only one <i>B. stacei</i> line included in the analysis, we cannot reach solid conclusions about the origin of the S subgenomes, though the topology of the <i>B. stacei</i> subtree also supports an ancestral <i>B. stacei</i> parent for Bhyb26 and Bhyb118-5 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Phylogenomic analysis of nuclear genomes and dating."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Phylogenomic analysis of nuclear genomes and dating.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="960" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Best maximum-likelihood <i>Brachypodium</i> phylogenomic tree based on 745,854 syntenically aligned nuclear SNVs. Most branches show strong support (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">6</a>). Divergence times for key branches are indicated based on the tree in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">7</a>. <i>B. stacei</i> (red), <i>B. distachyon</i> (blue), <i>B. hybridum</i> D plastotypes (S subgenome, brown; D subgenome, green), <i>B. hybridum-</i>S plastotype (S subgenome, yellow; D subgenome, purple). <b>b</b>, <b>c</b> Bayesian genomic structure analysis based on 5443 SNVs. Percentages of membership of genomes and subgenomes to the inferred genomic groups: <b>b</b> <i>B. stacei</i> and S subgenomes (<i>k</i> = 2), <b>c</b> <i>B. distachyon</i> and D subgenomes (<i>k</i> = 3). <b>d</b> Cross-bracing analysis to determine the dates of formation for the recent and ancient <i>B. hybridum</i> clades. The numbers on the diagonals represent the estimated divergence times of the D and S subgenomes from the diploid progenitor <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. stacei</i> genomic lineages.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We examined genetic structure using the set of 5443 SNVs described above. The <i>B. stacei</i>/S subgenomes formed two groups with the D-plastotype lines Bhyb26 and Bhyb118-5 being genetically isolated from the others (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4b</a>). The <i>B. distachyon</i> clade contained three groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4c</a>). Interestingly, LPA3.2 and CSR-6 were admixed with nearly equal proportion of the D plastotype and EDF + SNVs, indicating that these lines are more similar to the ancestors of the D-plastotype lines than the other <i>B. distachyon</i> lines. The D-plastotype D subgenomes were genetically isolated, whereas the S-plastotype D subgenomes showed introgression with the other groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4c</a>).</p><p>We dated the main splits of the <i>Brachypodium</i> tree using a coalescence model based on 4942 nucleotide positions. We calibrated the age of the <i>Brachypodium</i> crown node using a secondary constraint (normal prior mean 11.6 million years ago (Ma) SD 1.0) that encompassed previous fossil calibration-based nested dating<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Díaz-Pérez, A., López-Álvarez, D., Sancho, R. &amp; Catalán, P. Reconstructing the origins and the biogeography of species’ genomes in the highly reticulate allopolyploid-rich model grass genus Brachypodium using minimum evolution, coalescence and maximum likelihood approaches. Molecular Phylogenetics and Evolution 127, 256–271 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1911">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Sancho, R. et al. Comparative plastome genomics and phylogenomics of Brachypodium: flowering time signatures, introgression and recombination in recently diverged ecotypes. New Phytol. 218, 1631–1644 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1914">29</a></sup>. Within the <i>B. stacei</i> lineage, the S subgenomes of the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype clade diverged from its ancestral <i>B. stacei</i> lineage 1.5 Ma and the S subgenomes of the <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype clade from its parental <i>B. stacei</i> lineage 0.19 Ma. Within the <i>B. distachyon</i> lineage, the D subgenomes of the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype clade diverged from its ancestral <i>B. distachyon</i> lineage 1.1 Ma and the D subgenomes of the <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype clade from its parental <i>B. distachyon</i> lineage 0.14 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a</a>; Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">8</a>). In order to obtain a single-age estimate for the respective origins of both <i>B. hybridum</i> clades, we used a cross-bracing strategy that forces the split times of each parental genome to be contemporaneous<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="McCann, J. et al. Dating the species network: allopolyploidy and repetitive DNA evolution in American daisies (Melampodium sect. Melampodium, Asteraceae). Syst. Biolo. 67, 1010–1024 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1963">30</a></sup>. The D-plastotype clade formed at 1.4 Ma (95% HDP 1.1–1.6), and the S-plastotype clade formed at 0.14 Ma (95% HDP 0.11–0.18) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4d</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Plastome phylogenomics</h3><p>Plastome phylogenomic analysis was consistent with previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Sancho, R. et al. Comparative plastome genomics and phylogenomics of Brachypodium: flowering time signatures, introgression and recombination in recently diverged ecotypes. New Phytol. 218, 1631–1644 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d18949e1979">29</a></sup>, showing a main split for the <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i> clades (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig5">5</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">2a</a>). The <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype plastomes clustered with <i>B. stacei</i>, whereas the D-plastotype plastomes diverged earlier than the <i>B. distachyon</i> plastomes. Interestingly, two <i>B. hybridum</i> lines (Bhyb118-5 and Bhyb118-8) from the same location in southeastern Spain had different plastotypes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig5">5</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">2a</a>). Comparison of the <i>B. distachyon</i> nuclear and plastome trees revealed multiple chloroplast capture events (some previously reported) in all three groups, indicating a history of intercrossing between groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig5">5</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">2b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Phylogenomic analysis of plastid genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Phylogenomic analysis of plastid genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="460" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Best maximum-likelihood <i>Brachypodium</i> phylogenomic tree based on plastomes. Most branches of main clades show strong support (see Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">2a</a>). <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. hybridum-</i>S plastotypes (red); <i>B. hybridum</i> D plastotypes (olive); <i>B. distachyon</i> EDF + clade (blue); <i>B. distachyon</i> S + group (green); <i>B. distachyon</i> T + clade (yellow). Stars highlight plastid capture events between the <i>B. distachyon</i> groups (see also Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">2b</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Repetitive k-mer analysis</h3><p>We studied short repetitive DNA sequences, k-mers, as a proxy for transposable elements (TEs), to look for signatures of multiple independent polyploidization events in <i>B. hybridum</i>. We counted individual 26-mers in eight <i>B. distachyon</i> genomes, the <i>B. stacei</i> genome and eight <i>B. hybridum</i> genomes, two from D-plastotype lines and six from S-plastotype lines (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>). K-mers that were present in at least 100 copies in one genome with at least fivefold more copies in one pairwise comparison were fed into a machine-learning algorithm that grouped k-mers with similar enrichment patterns into seven different classes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">9a</a>). Remarkably, these classes fit expectations based on TE biology. Class 1 contains k-mers that are enriched in both subgenomes of the two D-plastotype <i>B. hybridum</i> lines (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6b</a>). This pattern is consistent with TEs that proliferated into both subgenomes after the ancestral polyploidization. This enrichment is not found in the S-plastotype lines, which supports separate polyploidization events for the two plastotypes. Classes 2 and 3 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6c</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">9b</a>) contain k-mers that are enriched in the <i>B. stacei</i> genome and all of the S subgenomes. This is consistent with repeats that proliferated in the <i>B. stacei</i> lineage after its split from the last common ancestor with <i>B. distachyon</i>, but before the formation of both <i>B. hybridum</i> plastotypes. That the D-plastotype lines have lower abundance in both classes suggests different <i>B. stacei</i> parental lines for each plastotype. Class 4 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6d</a>) is similar to classes 2 and 3, but the k-mers are nearly absent from the D-plastotype S subgenomes, indicating that the proliferation in <i>B. stacei</i> may have occurred after the polyploidization event that formed the D-plastotype <i>B. hybridum</i> lines. Class 5 k-mers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6e</a>) are enriched in all <i>B. distachyon</i> lines and D subgenomes. This is consistent with repeats that proliferated in the <i>B. distachyon</i> lineage after its split from the common ancestor with <i>B. stacei</i> and were then passed on to <i>B. hybridum</i>. Classes 6 and 7 (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6</a>f and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">9c</a>) highlight the intraspecific variation of these k-mers in <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. hybridum</i>. Overall, these results clearly indicate that <i>B. hybridum</i> arose at least twice, and are consistent with the nuclear and chloroplast phylogenetic trees (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig5">5</a>). In addition, the distinct k-mer patterns observed in the D and S-plastotype lines (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6b, d</a>) indicate that there has been little mixing between these groups.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="K-mer analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: K-mer analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1228" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> Three-dimensional kernel PCA (cosine kernel) plot showing the seven distinct classes of k-mers identified by the algorithm. Note the clear separation between classes. <b>b</b>–<b>f</b> Abundance of individual k-mers in each genome/subgenome are plotted for each k-mer class designated by color. The species, subgenome, and plastotype are indicated below the <i>x</i> axis. B.s. <i>B. stacei</i>. The classes correspond to expected histories of k-mer (repeat) abundance as follows: <b>b</b> repeats that expanded after the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype lines formed, but that did not expand in the S-plastotype lines (class 1); <b>c</b> repeats that proliferated in <i>B. stacei</i>, but not <i>B. distachyon</i> before the formation of all <i>B. hybridum</i> lines examined (class 2); <b>d</b> repeats that proliferated in <i>B. stacei</i> after the formation of the D-plastotype <i>B. hybridum</i> lines, but before the divergence of the S-plastotype <i>B. hybridum</i> lines (class 4); <b>e</b> repeats that proliferated in <i>B. distachyon</i>, but not in <i>B. stacei</i> before the formation of all <i>B. hybridum</i> lines examined (class 5); <b>f</b> repeats that show differential abundance in <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. hybridum</i> lines, suggesting <i>B. distachyon</i> founder effects (class 6). However, the lines with the highest abundance (Bd21, ABR113, Bd28) are the lines with very complete assemblies, so technical bias cannot be ruled out. <b>b</b>–<b>f</b> Dots represent relative abundance of individual k-mers. Boxplots show the median and 25–75% range. Error bars are + /− 1 sd. Plots for classes 3 and 7 are in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">9</a>. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Comparative pan-genomic analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: Comparative pan-genomic analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="459" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Comparison of the <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes to the <i>B. distachyon</i> pan-genome. <b>a</b> The number of genes that were found in all 52 <i>B. distachyon</i> lines (core genes with respect to <i>B. distachyon</i>) that were missing from each D subgenome. <b>b</b> The number of genes that were missing from each respective <i>B. distachyon</i> line but present in all other lines. These genes would have been core genes if that particular line had not been used to create the pan-genome. <b>c</b> The number of soft-core genes (found in 54–52 of all genomes/subgenomes) in each line. <b>d</b> The number of shell genes (found in 51-3 of all genomes/subgenomes) in each line. The <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype Bhyb26 line is indicated in green, the <i>B. hybridum-</i>S plastotype ABR113, Bhyb30, and Bd28 lines in pink, and the <i>B. distachyon</i> genomes in blue. Note that the <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes values fall within the <i>B. distachyon</i> genomes values. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Crossing ancestral and recent <i>B. hybridum</i> lines</h3><p>Multiple lines of evidence suggest that the <i>B. hybridum</i> D and S plastotypes are reproductively isolated. We experimentally tested their compatibility using controlled crosses and found that the D and S plastotypes are incompatible (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">10</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Pan-genomic analysis</h3><p>Previous analysis of the <i>B. distachyon</i> pan-genome revealed extensive presence/absence variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Extensive gene content variation in the Brachypodium distachyon pan-genome correlates with population structure. Nat. Commun. 8, 1–13 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2348">15</a></sup>. Thus, simply comparing a polyploid genome to single reference genomes from the progenitor species will greatly overestimate the changes that occurred after polyploidization. In order to differentiate changes that occurred after polyploidization from intraspecific differences in the progenitors, we conducted a pan-genomic analysis. We separated the D and S subgenomes of five <i>B. hybridum</i> lines and included them in a pan-genome analysis together with 51 <i>B. distachyon</i> lines and <i>B. stacei</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>). The coding sequence of genes (CDS) was clustered using the same method used to create the <i>B. distachyon</i> pan-genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Extensive gene content variation in the Brachypodium distachyon pan-genome correlates with population structure. Nat. Commun. 8, 1–13 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2368">15</a></sup>. The resulting matrix of gene clusters vs. lines (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM11">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM12">8</a>) was queried to compare the variation in the <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes to the variation among <i>B. distachyon</i> genomes. Overall, the D subgenomes looked like typical <i>B. distachyon</i> genomes in terms of the proportions of shell, soft-core and core genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig7">7</a>).
