We did note that the D subgenome from the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype line Bhyb26 was missing more <i>B. distachyon</i> core genes (genes found in all <i>B. distachyon</i> lines) than the other <i>B. hybridum</i> lines. To compare this to the intraspecific variation in <i>B. distachyon</i>, we determined how many pan-genes in each <i>B. distachyon</i> line were missing from only that line. Since these genes would have been core genes if the pan-genome was constructed without that particular line, this is the appropriate comparison with the number of <i>B. distachyon</i> core genes missing from the <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes. As the number of genes missing from only one <i>B. distachyon</i> line was similar to the number of core genes missing from the <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes, the core genes missing from the <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes as a whole could simply have been missing from the actual genomes of the <i>B. distachyon</i> parents of the polyploids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig7">7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Selection analysis</h3><p>Potential selection pressure effects were investigated in five <i>B. hybridum</i> lines, <i>B. stacei</i> and 44 <i>B. distachyon</i> lines (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>) using genes present in all lines as determined by the pan-genome analysis. Selection rates were estimated based on the dN/dS ratio over the full length of the CDSs in two groups of genes: one containing the <i>B. distachyon</i> lines and the <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes genes (D group) and the other containing <i>B. stacei</i> and the <i>B. hybridum-</i>S subgenomes genes (S group). In order to avoid phylogenetic bias, pairwise dN/dS comparisons were computed with respect to an outgroup: <i>B. stacei</i> for the D group and <i>B. distachyon</i> for the S group. Our results indicated similar levels of selection pressure on genes in the <i>B. hybridum</i> subgenomes and in the corresponding progenitor species genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">11</a>). Thus, we see no evidence of a release from negative selection pressure in the genes of the polyploids.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Reference-based analysis</h3><p>We conducted a reference-based analysis of the D subgenomes from eight <i>B. hybridum</i> lines and 114 <i>B. distachyon</i> lines (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>) to examine the spectrum of smaller structural changes (SNVs and small indels) and larger deletions and/or areas of high divergence relative to the <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 v3.1 reference genome. The analyses indicated that the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype lines were more divergent from <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 than the S-plastotype lines (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig8">8</a>). However, the S-plastotype D subgenomes were also significantly different (<i>t</i> test, <i>P</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−20</sup>) from the <i>B. distachyon</i> lines, indicating that while these D subgenomes are very similar to the <i>B. distachyon</i> genomes, they have accumulated numerous changes since the formation of the polyploids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig8">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Reference-based analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8: Reference-based analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig8_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-17302-5/MediaObjects/41467_2020_17302_Fig8_HTML.png" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="577" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>Individual Illumina sequence reads, or simulated reads created by shredding a PacBio assembly (Bd28 accession), were mapped to the <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 reference genome to identify SNVs (<b>a</b>), small indels (<b>b</b>), and larger missing or highly divergent regions (<b>c</b>). In each graph, the lines have been ordered by descending number of polymorphisms, showing a trend from high levels in the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype lines, moderate levels in the <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype lines, and low levels in the <i>B. distachyon</i> lines. For clarity, 105 intermediate <i>B. distachyon</i> lines were omitted from the graphs. The <i>B. hybridum</i> D and S-plastotype lines both have significantly more SNVs, indels and deleted or highly divergent regions than <i>B. distachyon</i> (<i>t</i> tests, <i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−20</sup>), whereas the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype lines had significantly more SNVs, indels (<i>t</i> test, <i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−9</sup>) and deletions or highly divergent regions (<i>t</i> test, <i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−4</sup>) than the S-plastotype lines. Note that the amount of deletion/highly divergent regions in Bd28 may be inflated by using a PacBio assembly that has more repetitive sequence assigned to the D subgenome than is possible by mapping Illumina reads. <b>d</b> The number of variants shared and unique to different groups. Green bars correspond to <i>B. hybridum</i> D plastotype, pink bars to <i>B. hybridum-</i>S plastotypes, and blue bars to <i>B. distachyon</i> accessions. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Interestingly, the <i>B. distachyon</i> lines that were closest to the D-plastotype lines in the phylogenetic tree and the structure analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a, c</a>) were also the most divergent from the reference <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 genome. The majority (73%) of the SNVs in the two <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype lines were not found in any <i>B. distachyon</i> line, whereas the converse was true for the six <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype lines, where only 30% of the variants were not shared with <i>B distachyon</i>, despite the fact that a larger number of S-plastotype lines were analyzed (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig8">8d</a>). This is consistent with a model in which the D-plastotype lines diverged earlier than the S-plastotype lines and over time accumulated novel mutations not found in <i>B. distachyon</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Expression analysis and pseudogenes</h3><p>Many polyploids exhibit homeolog-expression bias, where homeologs from one subgenome are systematically more highly expressed than homeologs from the other subgenome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Subgenome dominance in an interspecific hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and a 140-year-old naturally established neo-allopolyploid monkeyflower. Plant Cell 29, 2150–2167 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2648">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2651">32</a></sup>. RNA-seq in <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 leaves and spikes revealed that the distribution of gene expression values was very similar in each subgenome (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">2</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">12</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM13">9</a>).</p><p>The <i>B. hybridum</i> genome was scanned for pseudogenes, which are expected to accumulate in polyploids due to the relaxed natural selection that is believed to accompany genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2673">32</a></sup>. We examined regions of the <i>B. hybridum</i> genome where no gene was annotated, but where a gene was expected to exist based on synteny with the diploids. Only 24 candidate pseudogenes were identified. Twenty-two of the 24 pseudogenes were from the D subgenome. While this overrepresentation of D subgenome pseudogenes is intriguing, more study is needed to determine its biological significance. The dearth of high-confidence pseudogenes in <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 is consistent with our pan-genomic analysis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><p>Multiple lines of evidence indicate that <i>B. hybridum</i> arose from at least two distinct polyploidization events (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6</a>). This agrees with the fact that the D and S plastotypes were formed from reciprocal crosses between diploid progenitors (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig1">1</a>). Surprisingly, our k-mer, STRUCTURE, and phylogenetic analysis all indicate very little or no historical crossbreeding between these groups (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6</a>). This was particularly striking for Bhyb118-5 and Bhyb118-8 because they were collected from the same location. The incompatibility between D and S-plastotype lines in controlled crosses explains this genetic isolation. Thus, the different <i>B. hybridum</i> plastotypes may be cryptic species.</p><p>Dating analysis revealed that the S-plastotype lines formed ~0.14 Ma, and that the D-plastotype lines formed ~1.4 Ma. The very young ages of the S-plastotype lines (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4d</a>) is consistent with the nested positions of the S and D subgenomes within the respective <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i> clades and their almost contemporary ages with subgroups within <i>B. stacei</i> (0.19 Ma) and <i>B. distachyon</i> (0.096 Ma; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">7</a>). By contrast, the D-plastotype lines formed ~1.4 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4d</a>) and the sister position of its subgenomes in the <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. stacei</i> lineages supports the hypothesis of preferential survival of genomes in better adapted allopolyploids than in their potentially extinct diploid parents<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Madlung, A. Polyploidy and its effect on evolutionary success: old questions revisited with new tools. Heredity 110, 99–104 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2748">33</a></sup>. It is also possible that we have simply not sampled populations of <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. stacei</i> lines that are similar to the parents of the D-plastotype lines.</p><p>We found insertions of portions of the S plastome into the D plastome that could have resulted from heteroplasmy and recombination between ancestral D and S plastomes in the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype lines (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">3</a>). This is consistent with previous reports of heteroplasmy and plastid recombination in <i>B. distachyon</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Sancho, R. et al. Comparative plastome genomics and phylogenomics of Brachypodium: flowering time signatures, introgression and recombination in recently diverged ecotypes. New Phytol. 218, 1631–1644 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2770">29</a></sup>. While multiple founder events based on different plastotypes have been proposed for other allopolyploids, such as <i>Arabidopsis kamchatica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Novikova, P. Y., Hohmann, N., Van &amp; de Peer, Y. Polyploid Arabidopsis species originated around recent glaciation maxima. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 42, 8–15 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2776">34</a></sup>, plastid heteroplasmy has rarely been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Carbonell-Caballero, J. et al. A phylogenetic analysis of 34 chloroplast genomes elucidates the relationships between wild and domestic species within the genus citrus. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 2015–2035 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2781">35</a></sup>.</p><p>We observed no large structural variation between the <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 genome and the diploid reference genomes <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 and <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 both by overall synteny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig2">2a, b</a>) and by cytomolecular characterization (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">1</a>). This is similar to the high synteny observed between wheat subgenomes and may simply be due to the relatively recent formation of these polyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Appels, R. et al. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, 6403 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2807">36</a></sup>. However, since we did not have a highly contiguous assembly to evaluate synteny in a <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype line, we do not know the extent of large structural rearrangements in this older lineage. Our pan-genomic analysis identified thousands of differentially present genes between the reference <i>B. distachyon</i> genome and the <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes, however, the vast majority of this genic presence/absence variation was also present in at least some of the <i>B. distachyon</i> lines (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig7">7</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM11">7</a>). However, we cannot say that the same gene deletion/creation event is responsible for the loss of orthologous genes in any two lines at this time. Thus, there is no support for large-scale gene loss after polyploidization. This differs from the observation of some polyploid plant genomes where extensive genomic rearrangements and gene losses have been observed rapidly after polyploidization, especially in newly created <i>Brassica</i>, <i>Arabidopsis</i> and wheat allopolyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Chen, Z. J. &amp; Ni, Z. Mechanisms of genomic rearrangements and gene expression changes in plant polyploids. BioEssays 28, 240–252 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2836">37</a></sup>, but is similar to other allopolyploid plants with more stable genomes, such as synthetic allotetraploid cottons and wild allopolyploid <i>Spartina anglica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Chen, Z. J. &amp; Ni, Z. Mechanisms of genomic rearrangements and gene expression changes in plant polyploids. BioEssays 28, 240–252 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2843">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Salmon, A., Ainouche, M. L. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Genetic and epigenetic consequences of recent hybridization and polyploidy in Spartina (Poaceae). Mol. Ecol. 14, 1163–1175 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2846">38</a></sup>. Contrary to general expectations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Otto, S. P. The evolutionary consequences of polyploidy. Cell 131, 452–462 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2850">39</a></sup>, we did not detect significant overall differences in selection pressure on polyploid genes indicating that even after 1.4 million years homeologous genes face a similar selection pressure (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">11</a>). This is unlike the relaxation of purifying selection that was observed in the orthologous genes of the allohexaploid wheat genomes compared to those of their diploid parental genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Akhunov, E. D. et al. Comparative analysis of syntenic genes in grass genomes reveals accelerated rates of gene structure and coding sequence evolution in polyploid wheat. Plant Physiol. 161, 252–265 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2857">40</a></sup>. However, the wheat polyploidization events are much older<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Marcussen, T. et al. Ancient hybridizations among the ancestral genomes of bread wheat. Science 345, 6194 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2861">41</a></sup> than <i>B. hybridum</i>. We did observe large numbers of SNVs and small indels in the D-plastotype D subgenomes and a lesser, but highly significant, amount of polymorphism in the S-plastotype D subgenomes with respect to the <i>B. distachyon</i> reference genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig8">8</a>); based on k-mer distribution, we also found evidence for the movement of TEs between the D and S subgenomes in the old polyploids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig6">6b</a>).</p><p>We did not find any sign of genome dominance (an excess of homeolog-expression bias from one subgenome) in leaves and spikes. Genome dominance is typically correlated with the loss of sequence (fractionation) in the non-dominant genome(s)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2882">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Chang, P. L., Dilkes, B. P., McMahon, M., Comai, L. &amp; Nuzhdin, S. V. Homoeolog-specific retention and use in allotetraploid Arabidopsis suecica depends on parent of origin and network partners. Genome Biol. 11, R125 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2885">42</a></sup>. The genomic stability and equal transcriptomic contributions in the <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 subgenomes indicate that there is no sign of fractionation in this genome. This puts <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 in a class with several allopolyploids, which are structurally stable and show only subtle or no genome dominance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Douglas, G. M. et al. Hybrid origins and the earliest stages of diploidization in the highly successful recent polyploid Capsella bursa-pastoris. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 2806–2811 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2895">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the paleo-allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant 10, 1293–1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2898">44</a></sup>.</p><p>Taken together, our data support a scenario where the genes of <i>B. hybridum</i> evolve slowly after polyploidization with only small changes evident after a few hundred thousand years and a sizeable number of small polymorphisms evident after one million years, but no large amount of gene loss even after 1.4 million years. We did see evidence (k-mer profiles and lack of read mapping to sections of the reference genome) that repetitive DNA and other noncoding DNA is changing more rapidly than genes. Our results also highlight that allopolyploid species may in fact be composed of independent reproductively isolated clades formed at different times from different progenitors. This is consistent with other studies of wild allopolyploids where this may be the rule rather than the exception<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Novikova, P. Y. et al. Genome sequencing reveals the origin of the allotetraploid Arabidopsis suecica. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 957–968 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2908">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Shimizu-Inatsugi, R. et al. The allopolyploid Arabidopsis kamchatica originated from multiple individuals of Arabidopsis lyrata and Arabidopsis halleri. Mol. Ecol. 18, 4024–4048 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2911">46</a></sup>. <i>Brachypodium hybridum</i> is an extreme case because young and old clades occupy the same geographical site, yet remain genetically distinct.</p><p>The gradual changes we observed after polyploidization differ from many previous studies. However, it should be noted that we would have reached very different conclusions had we compared a single polyploid genome to single-progenitor diploid genomes. Only by taking a pan-genomic approach could we discern that the variation within the allopolyploid subgenomes was well within the intraspecific variation of at least one of the parents rather than a consequence of polyploidy per se.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Plant germplasm and sequencing</h3><p>The lines used in this study and their sources are described in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>, and a map of their origins is shown in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">4</a>. High-molecular-weight nuclear genomic DNA was isolated from 10–20 g of leaf tissue collected from 4-week-old seedlings and immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen. Tissue was finely ground into powder with mortar and pestle. DNA was isolated via a nuclei isolation protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Extensive gene content variation in the Brachypodium distachyon pan-genome correlates with population structure. Nat. Commun. 8, 1–13 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2939">15</a></sup>. First, frozen powdered tissue was resuspended by gentle swirling on ice in 250-mL beakers with sucrose extraction buffer containing beta-mercaptoethanol over a period of 20 min. Nuclei were filtered through a wide mesh cheese cloth to filter larger tissue fragments and further purified through multiple rounds of centrifugation in high-percentage sucrose buffer and subsequent decanting of supernatant. DNA was released to solution by digestion with Proteinase K followed by incubation with SDS. DNA was purified by multiple rounds of phenol:chloroform:isoamyl purifications followed by centrifugation until a clear aqueous/organic interface was observed. DNA was precipitated through addition of sodium acetate followed by an equal volume of isopropanol in microcentrifuge tubes. After 30-min incubation on ice, tubes were centrifuged at 4 °C for 30 min at 19,000 <i>g</i> in a tabletop microcentrifuge. Resulting pellets were washed by three rounds of 1 mL of ice-cold ethanol followed by centrifugation and decanting of the supernatant. Final pellets were briefly air-dried at room temperature and then resuspended in TE. Resulting DNA was prepared into library according to the manufacturer’s instructions. PacBio sequencing was performed on a PacBio RSII instrument. For Illumina sequencing, DNA was randomly sheared into desired fragment sizes, and then used to create Illumina fragment libraries or alternatively DNA was prepared according to the appropriate Nextera mate pair library protocol. Illumina sequencing was performed on Illumina HiSeq2500 and Illumina MiSeq sequencers at the Joint Genome Institute.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genetic mapping populations</h3><p>Genetic mapping populations were constructed for both <i>B. hybridum</i> and <i>B. stacei</i>. Lines ABR113 and BdTR6g of <i>B. hybridum</i> and ABR114 and TE4.3 of <i>B. stacei</i> were, respectively, crossed, and the F1 hybrids were confirmed using PCR markers. DNA was extracted from 200 F2 individuals from a single F1 hybrid in each case. Illumina fragment libraries were constructed for 192 samples for each mapping population. The <i>B. hybridum</i> and <i>B. stacei</i> populations were sequenced to 7× and 4× average coverage, respectively. For <i>B. stacei</i> 174 samples and for <i>B. hybridum</i> 167 F2 lines were sequenced to sufficient depth, and free from contamination to be included in the final genetic maps. Parental lines of respective mapping populations were sequenced to greater than 50× depth and non-repetitive 51-mers, in which the first 50 nucleotides were identical between both parents, and the last base contained a SNV distinguishing the parents were identified. Raw genotypes of individuals within mapping populations were determined by matching k-mers from the whole-genome skim sequencing of individuals to the markers described above. A given marker locus was determined as either homozygous for one of the parents if k-mers from only one of the respective parents was observed, or alternatively heterozygous if alleles for both parents were observed at a given marker locus. Raw genotypes were further processed into consensus genotypes for 7–14-kb fixed intervals across the draft assembly scaffolds by a simple majority rule algorithm amongst individual SNVs within each interval that required a given parental genotype to be observed at twice the frequency of the alternate parental allele and also twice the frequency of heterozygous calls. Likewise, a heterozygous consensus call was made if heterozygous genotypes were twice the frequency of both parental genotypes. Otherwise the consensus genotype was undetermined (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM6">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM7">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome assembly</h3><p>The <i>B. stacei</i> reference genome was created using the ALLPATHS (vLG)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513–1518 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d18949e2996">47</a></sup> assembler with 204× depth of Illumina reads from a combination of short fragment libraries and long-range mate pair libraries (1.5 kb, 4 kb, 7.5 kb). To validate and order scaffolds, a 22,043 marker genetic map was used. Assembly errors were identified by looking for simultaneous changes in haplotype across most F2 lines in each scaffold (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM6">2</a>). A total of six breaks were made in the original scaffolds. The scaffolds were then oriented, ordered, and joined together into ten chromosomes using a genetic linkage map produced by MST map (v1.0.0-2015)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Wu, Y., Bhat, P. R., Close, T. J. &amp; Lonardi, S. Efficient and accurate construction of genetic linkage maps from the minimum spanning tree of a graph. PLoS Genet. 4, e1000212 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3003">48</a></sup> from all 22,043 consensus markers. A total of 554 joins were made during this process.</p><p>Initial assembly of <i>B. hybridum</i> reference genome from inbred line ABR113 was performed using the Meraculous assembler (2.2.2.5 release) in diploid mode 2 in order to prevent collapse of subgenome sequences. The assembly utilized 28× of Illumina sequence from 250-bp paired-end sequences generated from an 800 bp-insert fragment-library and three long-range mate pair libraries (1 kb, 4 kb, and 7 kb). A genetic map was used to verify scaffolds as described for <i>B. stacei</i>, and two scaffolds were broken. The map was also used to order and orient the scaffolds to produce an interim assembly. The interim assembly was improved using additional Illumina sequence from Dovetail Genomic’s Chicago<sup>TM</sup> library and the HiRise<sup>TM</sup> assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342–350 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3020">49</a></sup>. During this process, 17 scaffolds were broken, 825 joins were made, and 64 gaps closed. The resulting scaffolds were essentially chromosome arms with a remarkable 15 Mb N50. The scaffolds were then oriented, ordered, and joined together into 15 chromosomes (504 Mb) using a genetic linkage map produced by MST map (v1.0.0-2015) using all consensus markers (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM7">3</a>). Final scaffolds were gap-filled yielding a final set of 8327 contigs within the 15 chromosomes (main genome contig N/L50: 1,128/135.1 kb). The final assembly was compared with the genetic map to verify order and orientation of sequences along the chromosomes.</p><p><i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> lines were assembled with the massively parallel HipMer genome assembler (v0.9.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Georganas, E. et al. HipMer: an extreme-scale de novo genome assembler. in Proceedings of the International Conference for High Performance Computing, Networking, Storage and Analysis) 1–11 (ACM, 2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3033">50</a></sup> on the Edison super-computer at the National Energy Research Scientific Computing Center, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory (USA). ALLMAPS (v0.7.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3037">51</a></sup> was used to order and orientate scaffolds based on synteny to the reference <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 genome.</p><p><i>Brachypodium hybridum</i> lines Bhyb30, Bhyb26, Bhyb118-5, Bhyb118-8, Bhyb123-6, and Bhyb127 were assembled from ~60× depth of Illumina paired-end reads using Meraculous assembler (2.2.2.5 release) as explained above. These assemblies were nearly complete, but fragmented (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM5">1</a>). Assembled scaffolds were ordered and orientated by alignment to the <i>B. hybridum</i> chromosome-level assembly of ABR113.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Annotation</h3><p><i>Brachypodium hybridum</i> lines ABR113, Bd28, Bhyb30, Bhyb26, and Bhyb118-5 as well as the <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 reference genome were annotated using the JGI annotation pipeline. EST assemblies were constructed from paired-end Illumina RNA-seq reads using PERTRAN (JGI/Phytozome internal pipeline) and PASA (v2.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3068">52</a></sup>. Loci were determined by transcript assembly alignments and/or EXONERATE (v2.4.0) alignments of proteins from <i>B. distachyon, B. stacei, A. thaliana</i>, rice, sorghum, foxtail, grape, soybean, and Swiss-Prot eukaryote proteins to the respective genome sequence, soft-repeatmasked using RepeatMasker (v4.0.5) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>), with up to 2-kb extension on both ends unless extending into another locus on the same strand. Gene models were predicted by homology-based predictors, FGENESH + (v2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3079">53</a></sup>, FGENESH_EST (v2.6) (similar to FGENESH+, EST as splice site and intron input instead of protein/translated ORF), and GenomeScan (v1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Yeh, R. F., Lim, L. P. &amp; Burge, C. B. Computational inference of homologous gene structures in the human genome. Genome Res. 11, 803–816 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3084">54</a></sup>. The highest scoring predictions for each locus were selected using multiple positive factors including EST and protein support, and one negative factor overlap with repeats. The selected gene predictions were improved by PASA. Improvement included adding UTRs, splicing correction, and alternative transcripts. PASA-improved gene model proteins were subject to protein homology analysis to the above mentioned proteomes to obtain Cscore and protein coverage. Cscore is a protein BLASTP score ratio to MBH (mutual best hit) BLASTP score and protein coverage is highest percentage of protein aligned to the best of homologs. PASA-improved transcripts were filtered based on Cscore, protein coverage, EST coverage, and its CDS overlapping with repeats. The transcripts were selected if their Cscore values were larger than or equal to 0.5 and protein coverage values larger than or equal to 0.5, or if they had EST coverage, but their CDS overlapping with repeats were less than 20%. For gene models whose CDS overlapped with repeats for &gt;20%, their Cscore values had to be at least 0.9 and homology coverage values of at least 70% to be selected. The selected gene models were subject to Pfam analysis, and gene models whose protein were more than 30% in Pfam TE domains were removed.</p><p>RNA-seq data (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM13">9</a>) from <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 and <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 and Bd28 inbred lines were used to aid the annotation of their respective genomes. ABR113 RNA-seq data were also used to aid the annotation of Bhyb26, Bhyb30, and Bhyb118-5 lines. A total of 55,011 transcript assemblies were produced by PASA, and were used for the annotation of the <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 genome. PASA transcript assemblies for ABR113 (199,500 transcript assemblies), Bhyb26 (124,003), Bhyb30 (126,716), Bd28 (111,639), and Bhyb118-5 (97,870) were also used for structural annotation of their respective genomes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM5">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Synteny analysis</h3><p>A phylogenetic tree for 54 <i>B. distachyon</i>, one <i>B. stacei</i>, and eight <i>B. hybridum</i> lines was constructed by running IQTREE v. 1.6.7 on the concatenation of Progressive Cactus multiple alignment of sequences for 100 bootstraps. Pairwise syntenic blocks were found via an internal JGI pipeline between all of the 99 <i>B. distachyon</i> and the eight <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes and eight <i>B. hybridum-</i>S subgenomes along with <i>B. stacei</i> (ABR114). Syntenic blocks were excluded if they had less than four syntenic loci. Pairwise syntenic blocks were merged together via <i>B. stacei</i> coordinates if they were within 300 kb. Sequences were extracted from each genome for the respective syntenic block, converted into multi-fasta files, and were formatted to run a multiple sequence alignment via Progressive Cactus (v0.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Paten, B. et al. Cactus: algorithms for genome multiple sequence alignment. Genome Res. 21, 1512–1528 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3140">55</a></sup>.</p><p>A separate Cactus whole-genome multiple sequence alignment of syntenic blocks that included rice, <i>B. distachyon</i>, and <i>B. stacei</i> was performed, and processed as described above. This is subsequently described as the outgroup whole-genome alignment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Karyotype analysis</h3><p>Cytomolecular mapping was done using comparative chromosome barcoding (CCB) on the following genotypes of <i>B. distachyon</i> (Bd21), <i>B. stacei</i> (ABR114, Bsta5 and ABR200), and <i>B. hybridum</i> (ABR113, ABR100 and ABR117) with 80 low-repeat <i>B. distachyon</i>-derived Bacterial Artificial Chromosome clones (BACs) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">1</a>). All BACs originated from the BD_ABa and BD_CBa genomic DNA libraries derived from the fingerprinted contigs, which had previously been assigned to the respective reference chromosomes of <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Febrer, M. et al. An integrated physical, genetic and cytogenetic map of Brachypodium distachyon, a model system for grass research. PLoS ONE 5, e13461 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3180">56</a></sup>. Chromosome preparation and fluorescence in situ hybridization followed the protocol described by Jenkins et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Jenkins, G. &amp; Hasterok, R. BAC ‘landing’ on chromosomes of Brachypodium distachyon for comparative genome alignment. Nat. Protoc. 2, 88–98 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3184">57</a></sup> with minor modification specific for the CCB approach performed according to Lusinska et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Lusinska, J. et al. Chromosome identification and reconstruction of evolutionary rearrangements in Brachypodium distachyon, B. stacei and B. hybridum. Ann. Bot. 10, 445–459 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3188">28</a></sup>. Young seedlings were incubated in a box with ice for 24 h, then fixed in 3:1 (v/v) 100% methanol/glacial acetic acid and stored at −20 °C until used. Excised root tips were washed three times for 5 min in 0.01 M citrate buffer (pH 4.8) to remove the fixative. Root tips of <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. hybridum</i> were digested in an enzyme mixture consisting of 8% (v/v) pectinase (Sigma), 1% (w/v) cellulase (Sigma), and 1% (w/v) cellulase Onozuka R-10 (Serva) in 0.01 M citrate buffer for 2 h at 37 °C. For <i>B. stacei</i>, the enzyme concentrations were 6%, 0.5%, and 0.5%, respectively, with the digestion time of 2 h 40 min at 37 °C. The meristems of each species were dissected in a small volume of 45% acetic acid followed by separate mounting of the digested material on a slide and squashed in drops of acetic acid. Squashed chromosome preparations were frozen thoroughly on dry ice followed by cover slip removal and allowed to air-dry.</p><p>BAC DNAs was isolated using the standard alkaline lysis method, and labeled by nick-translation mix with tetramethylrhodamine-5-dUTP, digoxigenin-11-dUTP, or biotin-16-dUTP (all Roche). Chromosome preparations were pre-treated with 100 μg/ml RNase A in 2 × SSC buffer for 1 h at 37 °C. Then, slides were washed three times for 5 min each in 2 × SSC buffer at room temperature (RT), fixed in freshly prepared 1% formaldehyde in 1 × PBS buffer for 10 min, washed three times for 5 min each in 2 × SSC, dehydrated in 70%, 90%, and 100% ethanol series and air-dried. For CCB, the hybridization mixture consisted of 40% deionized formamide, 15% dextran sulfate, 2 × SSC, 0.5% SDS, and 50–100 ng/ml of each DNA probe. Hybridization mix was denatured at 80 °C for 10 min, plunged into ice for 10 min and applied 40 µl to each slide. The slides were covered with plastic cover slips, and denatured in a humidity chamber at 70 °C for 4.5 min followed by incubation for ~40 h in a hermetically sealed humid chamber at 37 °C. Post-hybridization washes were equivalent to ~60% stringency. In brief, slides were washed twice in 20% (v/v) formamide in 2 × SSC for 5 min at 37 °C, rinsed three times for 5 min in 2 × SSC buffer at 37 °C followed by three changes of 2 × SSC, 5 min each, at RT. For immunodetection of digoxigenin- and biotin-labeled probes, slides were washed for 5 min at RT in 0.2% (v/v) Tween20 in 4 × SSC. Blocking reagent (5% solution of nonfat dry milk in 4 × SSC) was applied to each slide followed by incubation for 30 min at RT. Then, slides were drained and 40 µl of FITC-conjugated anti-digoxigenin antibodies (Roche) and Alexa Fluor 647-conjugated anti-biotin antibodies (Jackson ImmunoResearch) diluted 1:11 and 1:100, respectively, in blocking reagent were added to each slide, followed by incubation in a humid chamber for 2 h at 37 °C. Finally, slides were washed three times for 10 min each in Tween20/4 × SSC at 37 °C and dehydrated in ethanol series. Air-dried slides were mounted and counterstained in Vectashield (Vector Laboratories) containing 2.5 µg/ml of DAPI (4′,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole; Serva). Photomicrographs were acquired using an AxioImager.Z.2 (Zeiss) wide-field epifluorescence microscope equipped with high-sensitivity monochromatic camera (AxioCam Mrm; Zeiss) followed by standard digital processing using ZEN 2.3 Pro (Zeiss) and Photoshop CS3 (Adobe).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Plastome analysis</h3><p>Plastomes of 65 newly sequenced <i>B. hybridum</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i> lines were assembled from the total WGS data using NOVOPlasty v.2.7.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Dierckxsens, N., Mardulyn, P. &amp; Smits, G. NOVOPlasty: de novo assembly of organelle genomes from whole genome data. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, e18–e18 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3219">58</a></sup>. The complete and fully annotated plastomes of parental diploid species <i>B. distachyon</i> ABR6 (LT222229) and <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 (NC_036837; LT558589) were used as seeds for the assembly of the respective <i>B. distachyon</i>-type and <i>B. stacei</i>-type plastomes. All the new plastomes were assembled using a k-mer length of 39, insert size of 300 bp, and Illumina paired-end reads of 151 bp, except for <i>B. hybridum</i> Bd28 which was assembled from PacBio sequences using blasr v.5.3.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Chaisson, M. J. &amp; Tesler, G. Mapping single molecule sequencing reads using basic local alignment with successive refinement (BLASR): application and theory. BMC Bioinform. 13, 238 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3239">59</a></sup>, samtools v.1.4.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3243">60</a></sup>, and Canu v.1.7.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3247">61</a></sup> with corrected ErrorRate = 0.105 and batMemory = 50 parameters using the <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 plastome as a reference. Assembled plastomes were checked by visual inspection of read mappings using IGV v.2.3.8<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Thorvaldsdóttir, H., Robinson, J. T. &amp; Mesirov, J. P. Integrative genomics viewer (IGV): high-performance genomics data visualization and exploration. Briefings in Bioinform. 14, 178–192 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3255">62</a></sup>. The newly assembled plastomes of <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. hybridum-</i>S plastotypes were annotated using the <i>B. distachyon</i> ABR6 and <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 annotations as reference as described by Vu et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Novikova, P. Y. et al. Genome sequencing reveals the origin of the allotetraploid Arabidopsis suecica. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 957–968 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3272">45</a></sup>. The plastome of <i>B. hybridum</i> Bhyb26 (D plastotype) was annotated using cpGAVAS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Liu, C. et al. CpGAVAS, an integrated web server for the annotation, visualization, analysis, and GenBank submission of completely sequenced chloroplast genome sequences. BMC Genom. 13, (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3279">63</a></sup> and curated to be used as reference for annotation of the Bhyb118-5 plastome. The newly annotated plastomes of <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. hybridum</i> ecotypes were deposited at ENA (European Nucleotide Archive; <a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena">https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena</a>) with accessions numbers LR537446 – LR537510 (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>).</p><p>A multiple alignment of the newly assembled plastomes and of three previously annotated plastomes of <i>B. distachyon</i>, <i>B. stacei</i>, and maternal stacei-type <i>B. hybridum</i> plastomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Novikova, P. Y. et al. Genome sequencing reveals the origin of the allotetraploid Arabidopsis suecica. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 957–968 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3309">45</a></sup> was performed with MAFFT v.7.215<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772–780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3313">64</a></sup>. The multiple plastome alignment was filtered to remove poorly aligned regions through the automated option of trimAl v.1.2rev59 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Capella-Gutiérrez, S., Silla-Martínez, J. M. &amp; Gabaldón, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972–1973 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3318">65</a></sup> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM14">10</a>).</p><p>Noticeably, the <i>B. hybridum</i> D-plastotype plastomes share six insertions and common SNVs in two plastid regions in their predominantly <i>B. distachyon</i>-type plastid genomes with the <i>B. hybridum-</i>S plastotypes and <i>B. stacei</i> plastomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">3</a>). These plastid introgressions of the S plastome into the D plastome in Bhyb26 and Bhyb118-5 were probably ancient as none of the nuclear S and D subgenomes of the studied <i>B. hybridum</i> lines show evidence of introgressions between the old and young clades or backcrosses of the old hybrids to the <i>B. stacei</i> parent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Phylogenomic and genomic structure analysis</h3><p>In total, 745,854 SNVs were extracted from the multisequence alignment of in-group genomes (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM9">5</a>), described in the above synteny analysis section, into VCF format using Maffilter (v1.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Dutheil, J. Y., Gaillard, S. &amp; Stukenbrock, E. H. MafFilter: a highly flexible and extensible multiple genome alignment files processor. BMC Genom. 15, 53 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3364">66</a></sup> and MafStrander (v1.0) (<a href="https://github.com/dentearl/mafTools/tree/master/mafStrander">https://github.com/dentearl/mafTools/tree/master/mafStrander</a>). The VCF file was then converted into a multi-fasta and a final nexus file used in the nuclear phylogenomic analysis of in-group taxa only. This SNV set was intersected and merged with SNVs from the outgroup whole-genome alignment, yielding a final 5443 SNV VCF file that was converted into a multi-fasta and a final nexus file used in the nuclear phylogenomic analysis of in-group plus <i>Oryza sativa</i> as an outgroup (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM10">6</a>).</p><p>A nuclear maximum-likelihood (ML) optimal tree based on the 745,854 syntenic SNVs, from 116 genomes/subgenomes (99 <i>B. distachyon</i> genomes, 1 <i>B. stacei</i> genome, and 16 <i>B. hybridum</i> subgenomes), was constructed with IQTREE v. 1.6.7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Nguyen, L. T., Schmidt, H. A., Von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3390">67</a></sup>. The best-fit evolutionary model (TN + F + ASC + R6) was automatically selected by the ModelFinder option<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Urantówka, A. D., Kroczak, A. &amp; Mackiewicz, P. Complete mitochondrial genome of bronze-winged parrot (Pionus chalcopterus chalcopterus, Psittaciformes). Mitochondrial DNA B: Resour. 2, 744–746 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3394">68</a></sup> in IQTREE based on the Bayesian information criterion (BIC). The program computed 20 ML starting trees from 98 alternative randomized maximum parsimony (MP) trees, searching for best-scoring ML trees and estimating branch support for the best tree from 1000 bootstrap replicates using the ultrafast bootstrap option<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Hoang, D. T., Chernomor, O., Von Haeseler, A., Minh, B. Q. &amp; Vinh, L. S. UFBoot2: improving the ultrafast bootstrap approximation. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 518–522 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3399">69</a></sup>. Similarly, an optimal plastid ML tree based on the 108 aligned plastomes (99 from <i>B. distachyon</i>, one from B<i>. stacei</i> and eight from B. <i>hybridum</i>) based on 137,321 homologous plastid DNA positions (1782 parsimony-informative) was computed with IQTREE v. 1.6.7 using the same parameters as in the nuclear search and after inferring TVM + F + R4 as the best-fit evolutionary model for the plastid data. Both <i>Brachypodium</i> plastome and nuclear SNV trees were mid-point rooted, as this position was similar to that retrieved when using <i>O. sativa</i> to root the nuclear 5443 SNV-based topology (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">5</a>). The best nuclear and plastome ML trees were visualized with FigTree (v. 1.4.0) (<a href="http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree/">http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree/</a>).</p><p>Nuclear genomic structure among the highly divergent <i>B. stacei</i>-type (<i>B. stacei</i> plus <i>B. hybridum-</i>S subgenomes) and <i>B. distachyon</i>-type (<i>B. distachyon</i> plus <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes) lines was assessed with STRUCTURE (v. 2.3.4), imposing in each separate analysis an admixture ancestry model and a correlated allele frequencies model. We estimated values of genomic-group differentiation (K) between 1 and 4 for the <i>B. stacei</i>-type (S) group and between 1 and 7 for the <i>B. distachyon</i>-type (D) group, considering that up to two and up to five main genetic groups were detected respectively for each lineage in our phylogenetic ML analysis (see Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a</a>). In all cases, each search consisted of an initial burn-in of 10,000 Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) iterations followed by 100,000 additional MCMC iterations, and estimation of cluster membership (q) set to a 10% threshold value. Ten replicates were run for each K. The number of genomic groups (clusters) in the data was estimated using STRUCTURE HARVESTER (v. 0.9.94)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Earl, D. A. &amp; vonHoldt B. STRUCTURE HARVESTER: a website and program for visualizing STRUCTURE output and implementing the Evanno method. Conserv. Genet. Resour. 4, 359–361 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3457">70</a></sup>, which identifies the optimal <i>K</i> based both on the posterior probability of the data for a given K and the rate of change in the likelihood distribution among Ks (∆K)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Evanno, G., Regnaut, S. &amp; Goudet, J. Detecting the number of clusters of individuals using the software STRUCTURE: a simulation study. Mol. Ecol. 14, 2611–2620 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3465">71</a></sup>. The graphical outcomes were visualized using the software DISTRUCT (v. 1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Rosenberg, N. A. DISTRUCT: a program for the graphical display of population structure. Mol. Ecol. Notes 4, 137–138 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3469">72</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Dating and cross-bracing analysis</h3><p>Ancestral split times of the <i>B. stacei</i> and homeologous <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-type subgenomes’ groups and of the <i>B. distachyon</i> lineages and homeologous <i>B. hybridum</i> D-type subgenomes’ groups were estimated from a reduced nuclear dataset of 4942 SNVs and 41 tips representing all subgenomes of ancestral and recent <i>B. hybridum</i> plastotypes, <i>B. stacei</i>, the <i>B. distachyon</i> EDF + and earlier clades and selected clade members of the <i>B. distachyon</i> T + and S + groups (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>) using the SNAPP package of the BEAST 2.4.7 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Bouckaert, R. et al. BEAST 2: a software platform for Bayesian evolutionary analysis. PLoS Comput. Biol. 10, e1003537 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3509">73</a></sup>. We imposed several prior distributions: 1/x for the clock rate and lambda (Yule model), a uniform distribution for theta, and a normal distribution for a secondary age constrain at the <i>Brachypodium</i> crown node (mean = 11.6 Ma, SD = 1.0). The forward and backward mutation rates were fixed to give one expected mutation per unit of time. We ran 2,410,000 MCMC generations in SNAPP with a sampling frequency of 1000 generations, discarding the first 10% steps as burn-in. The adequacy of parameters was checked using TRACER v.1.6 (<a href="http://beast.bio.ed.ac.uk/Tracer">http://beast.bio.ed.ac.uk/Tracer</a>) with most parameters showing Effective Sample Size (ESS) &gt; 200. A maximum clade credibility tree was computed after discarding 10% of the respective saved trees as burn-in.</p><p>A Bayesian maximum clade credibility tree of 2170 posterior distribution trees showed a strongly supported divergence for the <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i> lineages that split from the common ancestor 11.5 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">7</a>). Noticeably, the divergence time of the D subgenomes of the <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype clade from its parental <i>B. distachyon</i> lineage (0.14 Ma) is less than the divergence between some of the <i>B. distachyon</i> groups (0.16 Ma; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a;</a> Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">7</a>). While the divergence times for the <i>B. distachyon</i> groups are confounded by introgression between groups, this only makes the recent divergence of the D subgenomes of the <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype clade more remarkable. The density tree cladogram showed a high congruence of most posterior distribution trees with this topology (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><p>The cross-bracing prior approach of McCann et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="McCann, J. et al. Dating the species network: allopolyploidy and repetitive DNA evolution in American daisies (Melampodium sect. Melampodium, Asteraceae). Syst. Biolo. 67, 1010–1024 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3565">30</a></sup> was adapted to SNAPP in order to obtain a single-age estimate for the respective origins of the ancestral (D plastotypes) and the recent (S plastotypes) clades of the <i>B. hybridum</i> allotetraploids. It was justified by the fact that the splits corresponding to the same hybridization events had nearly the same age (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#Fig4">4a</a>). We imposed a cross-bracing normal distribution prior with mean 0 and a standard deviation of 0.02, enforcing very low probability on trees that differ in node height and making the age distributions of the cross-braced nodes become nearly congruent with respect to mean and shape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="McCann, J. et al. Dating the species network: allopolyploidy and repetitive DNA evolution in American daisies (Melampodium sect. Melampodium, Asteraceae). Syst. Biolo. 67, 1010–1024 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3575">30</a></sup>. Prior distributions were generated for the split times between <i>B. stacei</i> and <i>B. hybridum</i>-S-subgenomes and for <i>B. distachyon</i> and <i>B. hybridum</i>-D-subgenomes and their arithmetic difference in two separate searches, one for the age of the ancestral <i>B. hybridum</i> D plastotypes and other for those of the recent <i>B. hybridum-</i>S plastotypes. We implemented the cross-bracing approach in SNAPP by manually adding several blocks containing parameter, prior, and operator commands in the Beauty xml file.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">K-mer analysis</h3><p>Transposable elements, TEs, turnover rapidly in genomes and can proliferate over short timescales making them useful for detecting the divergence between closely related genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Devos, K. M., Brown, J. K. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Genome size reduction through illegitimate recombination counteracts genome expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075–1079 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3606">74</a></sup>. Detecting different k-mer conservation rules between different strains can serve as a proxy to reconstruct the history of repetitive sequences (transposons and other types of repeats) among the lines and to elucidate differences in their recent evolutionary history. K-mers of length 26 were counted for eight <i>B. hybridum</i> lines, eight <i>B. distachyon</i> lines, and one <i>B. stacei</i> line (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>) using polyCRACKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Gordon, S. P., Levy, J. J. &amp; Vogel J. P. PolyCRACKER, a robust method for the unsupervised partitioning of polyploid subgenomes by signatures of repetitive DNA evolution. BMC Genom. 20, 580 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3623">75</a></sup>. Representative <i>B. distachyon</i> lines were selected based on dimensionality reduction and clustering of a k-mer minhash distance matrix found via sourmash (v2.0.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Brown, C. &amp; Irber, L. sourmash: a library for MinHash sketching of DNA. J. Open Source Softw. 1, 27 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3630">76</a></sup>. The most representative members of these clusters, closest to the centroids, were selected for the analysis along with the reference <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 genome.</p><p>The k-mers found after this clustering step were considered to be part of a particular k-mer conservation class, and were visualized and analyzed using an interactive Plotly Dash (v0.30.0) application. The relative distribution of k-mers across the studied lines of each analysis was shown on boxplots with their box points and a descriptive PCA plot detailing k-mer abundance for particular rules. The post-assembled distributions of k-mers were compared with their respective pre-assembled distributions, corroborating that their observed conservation patterns did not correspond to a failure to assemble repeats in certain assemblies or due to scaffolding artifacts. The retrieved conservation classes of the k-mers highlighted their abundance or sparsity along the different studied lines and subgenomes, illustrating the origins of these k-mers from the proliferation of other repetitive sequences.</p><p>While variation in the completeness of the genome assemblies can impact this analysis, we do not believe this affected our main conclusions for three reasons. First, 13 of the 17 lines analyzed were similar quality Illumina assemblies. Second, the higher-quality assemblies always grouped together consistently with the most closely related lines. For example, all six <i>B. hybridum-</i>S-plastotype lines show very similar patterns, despite the fact that Bd28 was a PacBio assembly, ABR113 was a high-quality reference assembly and the other four lines were Illumina assemblies. Third, the subgenomes in each <i>B. hybridum</i> line had very different k-mer patterns. That said, the relative abundance of k-mers in classes six and seven may be affected by assembly quality since the lines with the highest abundance of those k-mers, Bd21, Bd28, ABR113 are also the lines with the best assemblies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Pan-genome analysis</h3><p>CDS sequences from primary transcripts annotated in four of the newly assembled <i>B. hybridum</i> genomes (ABR113 reference genome, Bhyb26 D plastotype, and Bd28 and Bhyb30 S plastotypes; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>) were assigned to S and D subgenomes. The resulting FASTA files were analyzed with GET_HOMOLOGUES-EST v09052018<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Contreras-Moreira, B. et al. Analysis of plant pan-genomes and transcriptomes with GET_HOMOLOGUES-EST, a clustering solution for sequences of the same species. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 184 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3664">77</a></sup> together with CDS sequences from <i>B. stacei</i> (ABR114) and 52 <i>B. distachyon</i> lines. Nucleotide-based sequence clusters were produced with the MCL algorithm (-M) requiring alignment coverage of at least 75% (-C 75) and no sequence identity restriction (-S 1). The resulting clusters were then sorted, and a pan-genome matrix was produced summarizing the occupancy of each gene across the whole set of genomes and subgenomes. Finally, the pan-genome matrix was interrogated in order to obtain the number of core, soft-core and shell genes. Detailed protocols and sequence files are available at <a href="http://floresta.eead.csic.es/plant-pan-genomes/Bhybridum">http://floresta.eead.csic.es/plant-pan-genomes/Bhybridum</a>.</p><p>While assembly quality could affect the pan-genome analysis, we do not think this caused significant bias because low complexity sequences like genes are efficiently assembled in the compact <i>Brachypodium</i> genomes. In addition, the fact that the three higher-quality assemblies appear indistinguishable from the other assemblies indicates that assembly quality is not a systematic source of error.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Selection analysis</h3><p>Selection rates were estimated using dN/dS nucleotide substitution rate ratio in the D and S genic groups that contained 7228 and 9432 orthologs (CDSs), respectively (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM8">4</a>). The smaller S group (one <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 genome and five <i>B. hybridum</i> Bhyb26, Bhyb118-5, ABR113, Bd28, Bhyb30 S subgenomes) had more genes orthologous to the <i>B. distachyon</i> Bd21 outgroup genome than the large D group (44 <i>B. distachyon</i> genomes and 5 <i>B. hybridum</i> D subgenomes) to the <i>B. stacei</i> ABR114 outgroup genome. Pairwise dN/dS values were calculated for all samples and genes within each group using their respective outgroup sequence (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#MOESM15">11</a>). Basic statistics (mean, median, SD, range) of the dN/dS values were computed for each sample, and the total number of genes using the Rstat package v. 4.1.0 (R core team) and their distributions were plotted using box and whisker plots. Considering the nonnegative distribution of dN/dS values, statistical significant differences between median dN/dS values of samples within each D and S group were tested using a GLM test with a gamma distribution, which tests for significant differences, a Wilcoxon pairwise difference test for all pairs of samples, and a Kruskal–Wallis rank test for the whole group of samples within each group using the respective options of the Rstat package. Further statistical analyses were conducted using the lsmeans package of R to compute posthoc Tukey tests for the least square means.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Reference-based analysis</h3><p>Illumina reads for each line (except for Bd28 for which simulated Illumina short-reads were created from the final PacBio genome assembly) were aligned to Bd21-3 v1.1 reference genome with BWA (v0.7.17)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3726">78</a></sup>, filtered with Picard tools (v2.18) FixMateInformation and MarkDuplicates (<a href="https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard">https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard</a>), then GATK (v4.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="McKenna, A. et al. The genome analysis toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-17302-5#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d18949e3734">79</a></sup> was used for base-quality score recalibration, indel realignment, and SNV and InDel discovery using standard hard filtering parameters from GATK Best Practices recommendations. Areas with a read depth &lt;3 were counted as deletions or highly divergent regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Gene expression analysis</h3><p>RNA-seq data were obtained for <i>B. hybridum</i> ABR113 leaves and spikelets. Leaf samples were from seedlings at the 3–4 leaf stage. Each spikelet sample consisted of the spikelets from one plant, with each spikelet collected separately 3 days after inflorescence emergence.
